data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/20px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/40px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Scientific method</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_research&amp;redirect=no" title="Scientific research">Scientific research</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Scientific research" redirects here. For the publisher, see <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Research_Publishing" title="Scientific Research Publishing">Scientific Research Publishing</a>.</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Compare <a href="/wiki/Observational_study" title="Observational study">Observational study</a> and <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">Experiment</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a broader coverage of this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">Research</a>.</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Scientific_method_(disambiguation)" title="Scientific method (disambiguation)">Scientific method (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_Deutsch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="898" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/David_Deutsch.jpg/220px-David_Deutsch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/David_Deutsch.jpg/330px-David_Deutsch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/David_Deutsch.jpg/440px-David_Deutsch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_Deutsch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> with  <a href="/wiki/David_Deutsch" title="David Deutsch">David Deutsch</a>, proponent of invariant scientific explanations. <i>See <a href="#DNA_example">&#167; DNA example</a></i> below.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>scientific method</b> is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">empirical</a> method of acquiring knowledge that has characterized the development of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> since at least the 17th century. It involves careful observation, applying rigorous <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">skepticism</a> about what is observed, given that <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science#Observation_inseparable_from_theory" title="Philosophy of science">cognitive assumptions</a> can distort how one interprets the <a href="/wiki/Perception#Process_and_terminology" title="Perception">observation</a>. It involves formulating <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypotheses</a>, via <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">induction</a>, based on such observations; <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experimental</a> and measurement-based testing of <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductions</a> drawn from the hypotheses; and refinement (or elimination) of the hypotheses based on the experimental findings. These are <i>principles</i> of the scientific method, as distinguished from a definitive series of steps applicable to all scientific enterprises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though diverse models for the scientific method are available, there is in general a continuous <b>process</b> that includes observations about the natural world. People are naturally inquisitive, so they often come up with questions about things they see or hear, and they often develop ideas or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypotheses" title="Hypotheses">hypotheses</a> about why things are the way they are. The best hypotheses lead to predictions that can be tested in various ways. The most conclusive testing of hypotheses comes from reasoning based on carefully controlled experimental data. Depending on how well additional tests match the predictions, the original hypothesis may require refinement, alteration, expansion or even rejection. If a particular hypothesis becomes very well supported, a general <a href="/wiki/Scientific_theory" title="Scientific theory">theory</a> may be developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garland2015_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garland2015-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although procedures vary from one <a href="/wiki/Branches_of_science" title="Branches of science">field of inquiry</a> to another, they are frequently the same from one to another. The process of the scientific method involves making <a href="/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjectures</a> (hypotheses), deriving predictions from them as logical consequences, and then carrying out experiments or empirical observations based on those predictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NA_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NA-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> A hypothesis is a conjecture, based on knowledge obtained while seeking answers to the question. The hypothesis might be very specific, or it might be broad. Scientists then test hypotheses by conducting experiments or studies. A scientific hypothesis must be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falsifiable" title="Falsifiable">falsifiable</a>, implying that it is possible to identify a possible outcome of an experiment or observation that conflicts with predictions deduced from the hypothesis; otherwise, the hypothesis cannot be meaningfully tested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The purpose of an experiment is to determine whether <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observations</a> agree with or conflict with the predictions derived from a hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-8">[8]</a></sup> Experiments can take place anywhere from a garage to CERN\'s <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a>. There are difficulties in a formulaic statement of method, however. Though the scientific method is often presented as a fixed sequence of steps, it represents rather a set of general principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\nNot all steps take place in every scientific inquiry (nor to the same degree), and they are not always in the same order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gauch2003_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gauch2003-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inductive_Science_1837_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inductive_Science_1837-11">[11]</a></sup>  Some philosophers and scientists have argued that there is no scientific method; they include physicist <a href="/wiki/Lee_Smolin" title="Lee Smolin">Lee Smolin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Paul_Feyerabend" title="Paul Feyerabend">Paul Feyerabend</a> (in his <i><a href="/wiki/Against_Method" title="Against Method">Against Method</a></i>). Robert Nola and Howard Sankey remark that "For some, the whole idea of a theory of scientific method is yester-year\'s debate, the continuation of which can be summed up as yet more of the proverbial deceased equine castigation. We beg to differ."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872693506">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Post-modernism_and_science_wars"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Post-modernism and science wars</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Anthropology"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Anthropology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-49"><a href="#Role_of_chance_in_discovery"><span class="tocnumber">8.4</span> <span class="toctext">Role of chance in discovery</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#Relationship_with_mathematics"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Relationship with mathematics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-51"><a href="#Relationship_with_statistics"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Relationship with statistics</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-52"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-53"><a href="#Problems_and_issues"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Problems and issues</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-54"><a href="#History,_philosophy,_sociology"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">History, philosophy, sociology</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-55"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-56"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-57"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-58"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_scientific_method" title="History of scientific method">History of scientific method</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_history_of_scientific_method" title="Timeline of the history of scientific method">Timeline of the history of scientific method</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872446029">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .mobile-float-reset{float:none!important;width:100%!important}}.mw-parser-output .stack-container{box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-left{float:left;clear:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-clear-right{float:right;clear:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .stack-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .stack-object{margin:1px;overflow:hidden}</style><div class="mw-stack stack-container stack-right mobile-float-reset"><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2275" data-file-width="1700" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/150px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/225px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/300px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, 384&#8211;322 BCE. "As regards his method, Aristotle is recognized as the inventor of scientific method because of his refined analysis of logical implications contained in demonstrative discourse, which goes well beyond natural logic and does not owe anything to the ones who philosophized before him." &#8211; Riccardo Pozzo<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n</div><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hazan.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="289" data-file-width="245" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hazan.png/150px-Hazan.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Hazan.png/225px-Hazan.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f4/Hazan.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hazan.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> (Alhazen), 965&#8211;1039 <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. A polymath, considered by some to be the father of modern scientific <a href="/wiki/Methodology" title="Methodology">methodology</a>, due to his emphasis on experimental data and <a href="/wiki/Reproducibility" title="Reproducibility">reproducibility</a> of its results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n</div><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2060" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg/150px-Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg/225px-Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg/300px-Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Johannes_Kepler_1610.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> (1571&#8211;1630). "Kepler shows his keen logical sense in detailing the whole process by which he finally arrived at the true orbit. This is the greatest piece of Retroductive reasoning ever performed." &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sanders_Peirce" title="Charles Sanders Peirce">C.&#160;S. Peirce</a>, c. 1896, on Kepler\'s reasoning through explanatory hypotheses<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n</div><div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="1180" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/150px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/225px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/300px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> (1564&#8211;1642). According to <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>, "All knowledge of reality starts from experience and ends in it. Propositions arrived at by purely logical means are completely empty as regards reality. Because Galileo saw this, and particularly because he drummed it into the scientific world, he is the father of modern physics &#8211; indeed, of modern science altogether."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n</div></div>\n<p>Important debates in the history of science concern <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>, especially as advocated by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren&#233; Descartes">Ren&#233; Descartes</a>; <a href="/wiki/Inductivism" title="Inductivism">inductivism</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>, as argued for by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, and rising to particular prominence with <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> and his followers; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypothetico-deductivism" title="Hypothetico-deductivism">hypothetico-deductivism</a>, which came to the fore in the early 19th century.\n</p><p>The term "scientific method" emerged in the 19th century, when a significant institutional development of science was taking place and terminologies establishing clear <a href="/wiki/Demarcation_problem" title="Demarcation problem">boundaries</a> between science and non-science, such as "scientist" and "pseudoscience", appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thurs_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thurs-19">[19]</a></sup> Throughout the 1830s and 1850s, by which time Baconianism was popular, naturalists like William Whewell, John Herschel, John Stuart Mill engaged in debates over "induction" and "facts" and were focused on how to generate knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thurs_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thurs-19">[19]</a></sup> In the late 19th a debate over <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">realism</a> vs. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antirealism" title="Antirealism">antirealism</a> was conducted as powerful scientific theories extended beyond the realm of the observable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term "scientific method" came into popular use in the twentieth century, popping up in dictionaries and science textbooks, although there was little scientific consensus over its meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thurs_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thurs-19">[19]</a></sup> Although there was a growth through the middle of the twentieth century, by the end of that century numerous influential philosophers of science like Thomas Kuhn and Paul Feyerabend had questioned the universality of the "scientific method" and in doing so largely replaced the notion of science as a homogeneous and universal method with that of it being a heterogeneous and local practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thurs_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Thurs-19">[19]</a></sup> In particular, Paul Feyerabend argued against there being any universal rules of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">Historian of science</a> Daniel Thurs maintains that the scientific method is a myth or, at best, an idealization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">The <a href="#DNA_example">DNA example</a> below is a synopsis of this method.</div>\n<p>The scientific method is the process by which <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> is carried out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> As in other areas of inquiry, science (through the scientific method) can build on previous knowledge and develop a more sophisticated understanding of its topics of study over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fixation_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fixation-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vital_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vital-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anti-fragility_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anti-fragility-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> This model can be seen to underlie the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ubiquitous element in the model of the scientific method is <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>, or more precisely, epistemologic <a href="/wiki/Sensualism" title="Sensualism">sensualism</a>. This is in opposition to stringent forms of <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>: the scientific method embodies that reason alone cannot solve a particular scientific problem. A strong formulation of the scientific method is not always aligned with a form of <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a> in which the empirical data is put forward in the form of experience or other abstracted forms of knowledge; in current scientific practice, however, the use of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_modelling" title="Scientific modelling">scientific modelling</a> and reliance on abstract typologies and theories is normally accepted. The scientific method is of necessity also an expression of an opposition to claims that e.g. <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a>, political or religious <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a>, appeals to tradition, commonly held beliefs, common sense, or, importantly, currently held theories, are the only possible means of demonstrating truth.\n</p><p>Different early expressions of empiricism and the scientific method can be found throughout history, for instance with the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stoics" title="Stoics">Stoics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asmis_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asmis-30">[30]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alhazen_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alhazen-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>. From the 16th century onwards, experiments were advocated by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, and performed by <a href="/wiki/Giambattista_della_Porta" title="Giambattista della Porta">Giambattista della Porta</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>  There was particular development  aided by theoretical works by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Sanches" title="Francisco Sanches">Francisco Sanches</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">George Berkeley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hypothetico-deductive_model" title="Hypothetico-deductive model">hypothetico-deductive model</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> formulated in the 20th century, is the ideal although it has undergone significant revision since first proposed (for a more formal discussion, see <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method#Elements_of_the_scientific_method" title="Scientific method">below</a>). Staddon (2017) argues it is a mistake to try following rules<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staddon2017_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staddon2017-37">[37]</a></sup> which are best learned through careful study of examples of scientific investigation. \n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Process">Process</span></h3>\n<p>The overall process involves making <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjectures" title="Conjectures">conjectures</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypotheses</a>), deriving predictions from them as logical consequences, and then carrying out experiments based on those predictions to determine whether the original <a href="/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjecture</a> was correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NA_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-NA-5">[5]</a></sup> There are difficulties in a formulaic statement of method, however. Though the scientific method is often presented as a fixed sequence of steps, these actions are better considered as general principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gauch2003_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gauch2003-10">[10]</a></sup> Not all steps take place in every scientific inquiry (nor to the same degree), and they are not always done in the same order. As noted by scientist and philosopher <a href="/wiki/William_Whewell" title="William Whewell">William Whewell</a> (1794&#8211;1866), "invention, sagacity, [and] genius"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inductive_Science_1837_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Inductive_Science_1837-11">[11]</a></sup> are required at every step.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Formulation_of_a_question">Formulation of a question</span></h4>\n<p>The question can refer to the explanation of a specific <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observation</a>, as in "Why is the sky blue?" but can also be open-ended, as in "How can I <a href="/wiki/Drug_design" title="Drug design">design a drug</a> to cure this particular disease?" This stage frequently involves finding and evaluating evidence from previous experiments, personal scientific observations or assertions, as well as the work of other scientists. If the answer is already known, a different question that builds on the evidence can be posed. When applying the scientific method to research, determining a good question can be very difficult and it will affect the outcome of the investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypothesis">Hypothesis</span></h4>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjecture</a>, based on knowledge obtained while formulating the question, that may explain any given behavior. The hypothesis might be very specific; for example, Einstein\'s <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">equivalence principle</a> or <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a>\'s "DNA makes RNA makes protein",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> or it might be broad; for example, unknown species of life dwell in the unexplored depths of the oceans. A <a href="/wiki/Statistical_hypothesis_testing#Definition_of_terms" title="Statistical hypothesis testing">statistical hypothesis</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjecture</a> about a given <a href="/wiki/Statistical_population" title="Statistical population">statistical population</a>. For example, the population might be <i>people with a particular disease.</i> The conjecture might be that a new drug will cure the disease in some of those people. Terms commonly associated with statistical hypotheses are <a href="/wiki/Null_hypothesis" title="Null hypothesis">null hypothesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alternative_hypothesis" title="Alternative hypothesis">alternative hypothesis</a>. A null hypothesis is the conjecture that the statistical hypothesis is false; for example, that the new drug does nothing and that any cure is caused by <a href="/wiki/Luck" title="Luck">chance</a>. Researchers normally want to show that the null hypothesis is false. The alternative hypothesis is the desired outcome, that the drug does better than chance. A final point: a scientific hypothesis must be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falsifiable" title="Falsifiable">falsifiable</a>, meaning that one can identify a possible outcome of an experiment that conflicts with predictions deduced from the hypothesis; otherwise, it cannot be meaningfully tested.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prediction">Prediction</span></h4>\n<p>This step involves determining the logical consequences of the hypothesis. One or more predictions are then selected for further testing. The more unlikely that a prediction would be correct simply by coincidence, then the more convincing it would be if the prediction were fulfilled; evidence is also stronger if the answer to the prediction is not already known, due to the effects of <a href="/wiki/Hindsight_bias" title="Hindsight bias">hindsight bias</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Postdiction" title="Postdiction">postdiction</a>). Ideally, the prediction must also distinguish the hypothesis from likely alternatives; if two hypotheses make the same prediction, observing the prediction to be correct is not evidence for either one over the other. (These statements about the relative strength of evidence can be mathematically derived using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayes%27_Theorem" title="Bayes\' Theorem">Bayes\' Theorem</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Testing">Testing</span></h4>\n<p>This is an investigation of whether the real world behaves as predicted by the hypothesis. Scientists (and other people) test hypotheses by conducting <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>. The purpose of an experiment is to determine whether <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observations" title="Observations">observations</a> of the real world agree with or conflict with the predictions derived from a hypothesis. If they agree, confidence in the hypothesis increases; otherwise, it decreases. Agreement does not assure that the hypothesis is true; future experiments may reveal problems. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a> advised scientists to try to falsify hypotheses, i.e., to search for and test those experiments that seem most doubtful. Large numbers of successful confirmations are not convincing if they arise from experiments that avoid risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-8">[8]</a></sup> Experiments should be designed to minimize possible errors, especially through the use of appropriate <a href="/wiki/Scientific_control" title="Scientific control">scientific controls</a>. For example, tests of medical treatments are commonly run as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blind_experiment#Double-blind_trials" title="Blind experiment">double-blind tests</a>. Test personnel, who might unwittingly reveal to test subjects which samples are the desired test drugs and which are <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebos</a>, are kept ignorant of which are which. Such hints can bias the responses of the test subjects. Furthermore, failure of an experiment does not necessarily mean the hypothesis is false. Experiments always depend on several hypotheses, e.g., that the test equipment is working properly, and a failure may be a failure of one of the auxiliary hypotheses. (See the <a href="/wiki/Duhem%E2%80%93Quine_thesis" title="Duhem&#8211;Quine thesis">Duhem&#8211;Quine thesis</a>.) Experiments can be conducted in a college lab, on a kitchen table, at CERN\'s <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">Large Hadron Collider</a>, at the bottom of an ocean, on Mars (using one of the working <a href="/wiki/Mars_rover" title="Mars rover">rovers</a>), and so on. Astronomers do experiments, searching for planets around distant stars. Finally, most individual experiments address highly specific topics for reasons of practicality. As a result, evidence about broader topics is usually accumulated gradually.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span></h4>\n<p>This involves determining what the results of the experiment show and deciding on the next actions to take. The predictions of the hypothesis are compared to those of the null hypothesis, to determine which is better able to explain the data. In cases where an experiment is repeated many times, a <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistical analysis</a> such as a <a href="/wiki/Chi-squared_test" title="Chi-squared test">chi-squared test</a> may be required. If the evidence has falsified the hypothesis, a new hypothesis is required; if the experiment supports the hypothesis but the evidence is not strong enough for high confidence, other predictions from the hypothesis must be tested. Once a hypothesis is strongly supported by evidence, a new question can be asked to provide further insight on the same topic. Evidence from other scientists and experience are frequently incorporated at any stage in the process. Depending on the complexity of the experiment, many iterations may be required to gather sufficient evidence to answer a question with confidence, or to build up many answers to highly specific questions in order to answer a single broader question.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_example">DNA example</span></h3>\n<p><img alt="" data-file-height="26" data-file-width="25" decoding="async" height="26" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/DNA_icon_%2825x25%29.png" width="25"/>\nThe basic <a href="#Elements_of_the_scientific_method">elements of the scientific method</a> are illustrated by the following example from the discovery of the structure of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i><a href="#DNA-characterizations">Question</a></i>: Previous investigation of DNA had determined its chemical composition (the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleotides" title="Nucleotides">nucleotides</a>), the structure of each individual nucleotide, and other properties. It had been identified as the carrier of genetic information by the <a href="/wiki/Avery%E2%80%93MacLeod%E2%80%93McCarty_experiment" title="Avery&#8211;MacLeod&#8211;McCarty experiment">Avery&#8211;MacLeod&#8211;McCarty experiment</a> in 1944,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvnb|McCarty1985_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-harvnb|McCarty1985-41">[41]</a></sup> but the mechanism of how genetic information was stored in DNA was unclear.</li>\n<li><i><a href="#DNA-hypotheses">Hypothesis</a></i>: <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_D._Watson" title="James D. Watson">James D. Watson</a> hypothesized that DNA had a helical structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a href="#DNA-predictions">Prediction</a></i>: If DNA had a helical structure, its X-ray diffraction pattern would be X-shaped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crick_pp._137&#8211;138_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crick_pp._137&#8211;138-44">[44]</a></sup> This prediction was determined using the mathematics of the helix transform, which had been derived by Cochran, Crick and Vand<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HelixTransform_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-HelixTransform-45">[45]</a></sup> (and independently by Stokes). This prediction was a mathematical construct, completely independent from the biological problem at hand.</li>\n<li><i><a href="#DNA-experiments">Experiment</a></i>: <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a> crystallized pure DNA and performed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Photo_51" title="Photo 51">photo 51</a>. The results showed an X-shape.</li>\n<li><i><a href="#DNA-iterations">Analysis</a></i>: When Watson saw the detailed diffraction pattern, he immediately recognized it as a helix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeaTime_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-TeaTime-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_1968_167_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_1968_167-47">[47]</a></sup> He and Crick then produced their model, using this information along with the previously known information about DNA\'s composition and about molecular interactions such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonds" title="Hydrogen bonds">hydrogen bonds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SameShape_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-SameShape-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>The discovery became the starting point for many further studies involving the genetic material, such as the field of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular genetics</a>, and it was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize</a> in 1962. Each step of the example is examined in more detail later in the article.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_components">Other components</span></h3>\n<p>The scientific method also includes other components required even when all the iterations of the steps above have been completed:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Replication">Replication</span></h4>\n<p>If an experiment cannot be <a href="/wiki/Reproducibility" title="Reproducibility">repeated</a> to produce the same results, this implies that the original results might have been in error. As a result, it is common for a single experiment to be performed multiple times, especially when there are uncontrolled variables or other indications of <a href="/wiki/Observational_error" title="Observational error">experimental error</a>. For significant or surprising results, other scientists may also attempt to replicate the results for themselves, especially if those results would be important to their own work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="External_review">External review</span></h4>\n<p>The process of <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer review</a> involves evaluation of the experiment by experts, who typically give their opinions anonymously. Some journals request that the experimenter provide lists of possible peer reviewers, especially if the field is highly specialized. Peer review does not certify correctness of the results, only that, in the opinion of the reviewer, the experiments themselves were sound (based on the description supplied by the experimenter). If the work passes peer review, which occasionally may require new experiments requested by the reviewers, it will be published in a peer-reviewed <a href="/wiki/Academic_journal" title="Academic journal">scientific journal</a>. The specific journal that publishes the results indicates the perceived quality of the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_recording_and_sharing">Data recording and sharing</span></h4>\n<p>Scientists typically are careful in recording their data, a requirement promoted by <a href="/wiki/Ludwik_Fleck" title="Ludwik Fleck">Ludwik Fleck</a> (1896&#8211;1961) and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Though not typically required, they might be requested to <a href="/wiki/Data_sharing" title="Data sharing">supply this data</a> to other scientists who wish to replicate their original results (or parts of their original results), extending to the sharing of any experimental samples that may be difficult to obtain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_inquiry">Scientific inquiry</span></h2>\n<p>Scientific inquiry generally aims to obtain <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_theory" title="Scientific theory">testable explanations</a> that scientists can use to\n<a href="/wiki/Predictability" title="Predictability">predict</a> the results of future experiments. This allows scientists to gain a better understanding of the topic under study, and later to use that understanding to intervene in its causal mechanisms (such as to cure disease). The better an explanation is at making predictions, the more useful it frequently can be, and the more likely it will continue to explain a body of evidence better than its alternatives. The most successful explanations &#8211; those which explain and make accurate predictions in a wide range of circumstances &#8211; are often called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_theories" title="Scientific theories">scientific theories</a>.\n</p><p>Most experimental results do not produce large changes in human understanding; improvements in theoretical scientific understanding typically result from a gradual process of development over time, sometimes across different domains of science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Scientific models vary in the extent to which they have been experimentally tested and for how long, and in their acceptance in the scientific community. In general, explanations become accepted over time as evidence accumulates on a given topic, and the explanation in question proves more powerful than its alternatives at explaining the evidence. Often subsequent researchers re-formulate the explanations over time, or combined explanations to produce new explanations.\n</p><p>Tow sees the scientific method in terms of an <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_algorithm" title="Evolutionary algorithm">evolutionary algorithm</a> applied to science and technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_of_scientific_inquiry">Properties of scientific inquiry</span></h3>\n<p>Scientific knowledge is closely tied to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">empirical</a> findings, and can remain subject to <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">falsification</a> if new experimental observation incompatible with it is found. That is, no theory can ever be considered final, since new problematic evidence might be discovered. If such evidence is found, a new theory may be proposed, or (more commonly) it is found that modifications to the previous theory are sufficient to explain the new evidence. The strength of a theory can be argued<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to relate to how long it has persisted without major alteration to its core principles.\n</p><p>Theories can also become subsumed by other theories. For example, Newton\'s laws explained thousands of years of scientific observations of the planets   <a href="#Another_example:_precession_of_Mercury">almost perfectly</a>. However, these laws were then determined to be special cases of a more general theory (<a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">relativity</a>), which explained both the (previously unexplained) exceptions to Newton\'s laws and predicted and explained other observations such as the deflection of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> by <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. Thus, in certain cases independent, unconnected, scientific observations can be connected to each other, unified by principles of increasing explanatory power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brody_1993_44&#8211;45_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brody_1993_44&#8211;45-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldhaber_2010_page=942_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldhaber_2010_page=942-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since new theories might be more comprehensive than what preceded them, and thus be able to explain more than previous ones, successor theories might be able to meet a higher standard by explaining a larger body of observations than their predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brody_1993_44&#8211;45_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brody_1993_44&#8211;45-56">[56]</a></sup> For example, the theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> explains the <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">diversity of life on Earth</a>, how species adapt to their environments, and many other patterns observed in the natural world;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall08_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall08-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cracraft05_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cracraft05-59">[59]</a></sup> its most recent major modification was unification with <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Extended_evolutionary_synthesis" title="Extended evolutionary synthesis">modern evolutionary synthesis</a>. In subsequent modifications, it has also subsumed aspects of many other fields such as <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs_and_biases">Beliefs and biases</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Louis_Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2248" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Jean_Louis_Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault_001.jpg/220px-Jean_Louis_Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Jean_Louis_Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault_001.jpg/330px-Jean_Louis_Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Jean_Louis_Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault_001.jpg/440px-Jean_Louis_Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Louis_Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flying gallop as shown by this painting (<a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9odore_G%C3%A9ricault" title="Th&#233;odore G&#233;ricault">Th&#233;odore G&#233;ricault</a>, 1821) is <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">falsified</a>; see image below</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Horse_in_Motion_high_res.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3510" data-file-width="5694" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_Horse_in_Motion_high_res.jpg/220px-The_Horse_in_Motion_high_res.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_Horse_in_Motion_high_res.jpg/330px-The_Horse_in_Motion_high_res.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/The_Horse_in_Motion_high_res.jpg/440px-The_Horse_in_Motion_high_res.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Horse_in_Motion_high_res.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sallie_Gardner_at_a_Gallop" title="Sallie Gardner at a Gallop">Muybridge\'s photographs</a> of <i>The Horse in Motion,</i> 1878, were used to answer the question whether all four feet of a galloping horse are ever off the ground at the same time. This demonstrates a use of photography as an experimental tool in science.</div></div></div>\n<p>Scientific methodology often directs that <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypotheses</a> be tested in <a href="/wiki/Scientific_control" title="Scientific control">controlled</a> conditions wherever possible. This is frequently possible in certain areas, such as in the biological sciences, and more difficult in other areas, such as in astronomy.\n</p><p>The practice of experimental control and reproducibility can have the effect of diminishing the potentially harmful effects of circumstance, and to a degree, personal bias. For example, pre-existing beliefs can alter the interpretation of results, as in <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_bias" title="Confirmation bias">confirmation bias</a>; this is a <a href="/wiki/Heuristic" title="Heuristic">heuristic</a> that leads a person with a particular belief to see things as reinforcing their belief, even if another observer might disagree (in other words, people tend to observe what they expect to observe).\n</p><p>A historical example is the belief that the legs of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_gallop" title="Horse gallop">galloping</a> horse are splayed at the point when none of the horse\'s legs touches the ground, to the point of this image being included in paintings by its supporters. However, the first stop-action pictures of a horse\'s gallop by <a href="/wiki/Eadweard_Muybridge" title="Eadweard Muybridge">Eadweard Muybridge</a> showed this to be false, and that the legs are instead gathered together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another important human bias that plays a role is a preference for new, surprising statements (see <a href="/wiki/Appeal_to_novelty" title="Appeal to novelty">appeal to novelty</a>), which can result in a search for evidence that the new is true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldhaber_2010_page=940_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldhaber_2010_page=940-61">[61]</a></sup>  Poorly attested beliefs can be believed and acted upon via a less rigorous heuristic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Goldhaber and Nieto published in 2010 the observation that if theoretical structures with "many closely neighboring subjects are described by connecting theoretical concepts then the theoretical structure .. becomes increasingly hard to overturn".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldhaber_2010_page=942_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldhaber_2010_page=942-57">[57]</a></sup> When a narrative is constructed its elements become easier to believe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> For more on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narrative_fallacy" title="Narrative fallacy">narrative fallacy</a>, see also <a href="#CITEREFFleck1979">Fleck 1979</a>, p.&#160;27: "Words and ideas are originally phonetic and mental equivalences of the experiences coinciding with them. ... Such proto-ideas are at first always too broad and insufficiently specialized. ... Once a structurally complete and closed system of opinions consisting of many details and relations has been formed, it offers enduring resistance to anything that contradicts it." Sometimes, these have their elements assumed <a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori"><i>a priori</i></a>, or contain some other logical or methodological flaw in the process that ultimately produced them. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donald_M._MacKay" title="Donald M. MacKay">Donald M. MacKay</a> has analyzed these elements in terms of limits to the accuracy of measurement and has related them to instrumental elements in a category of measurement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elements_of_the_scientific_method">Elements of the scientific method</span></h2>\n<p>There are different ways of outlining the basic method used for scientific inquiry. The <a href="/wiki/Scientific_community" title="Scientific community">scientific community</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophers_of_science" title="Philosophers of science">philosophers of science</a> generally agree on the following classification of method components. These methodological elements and organization of procedures tend to be more characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural sciences</a> than <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a>. Nonetheless, the cycle of formulating hypotheses, testing and analyzing the results, and formulating new hypotheses, will resemble the cycle described below.\n</p><p>The scientific method is an iterative, cyclical process through which information is continually revised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> It is generally recognized to develop advances in knowledge through the following elements, in varying combinations or contributions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhn50_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhn50-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galison_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galison-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Characterizations (observations, definitions, and measurements of the subject of inquiry)</li>\n<li>Hypotheses (theoretical, hypothetical explanations of observations and measurements of the subject)</li>\n<li>Predictions (inductive and deductive reasoning from the hypothesis or theory)</li>\n<li>Experiments (tests of all of the above)</li></ul>\n<p>Each element of the scientific method is subject to <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer review</a> for possible mistakes. These activities do not describe all that scientists do (<a href="#Dimensions_of_practice">see below</a>) but apply mostly to experimental sciences (e.g., physics, chemistry, and biology). The elements above are often taught in <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">the educational system</a> as "the scientific method".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The scientific method is not a single recipe: it requires intelligence, imagination, and creativity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In this sense, it is not a mindless set of standards and procedures to follow,\nbut is rather an <a href="#Evaluation_and_improvement">ongoing cycle</a>, constantly developing more useful, accurate and comprehensive models and methods. For example, when Einstein developed the Special and General Theories of Relativity, he did not in any way refute or discount Newton\'s <i>Principia</i>. On the contrary, if the astronomically large, the vanishingly small, and the extremely fast are removed from Einstein\'s theories &#8211; all phenomena Newton could not have observed &#8211; Newton\'s equations are what remain. Einstein\'s theories are expansions and refinements of Newton\'s theories and, thus, increase confidence in Newton\'s work.\n</p><p>A linearized, pragmatic scheme of the four points above is sometimes offered as a guideline for proceeding:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li>Define a question</li>\n<li>Gather information and resources (observe)</li>\n<li>Form an explanatory hypothesis</li>\n<li>Test the hypothesis by performing an experiment and collecting data in a <a href="/wiki/Reproducibility" title="Reproducibility">reproducible</a> manner</li>\n<li>Analyze the data</li>\n<li>Interpret the data and draw conclusions that serve as a starting point for new hypothesis</li>\n<li>Publish results</li>\n<li>Retest (frequently done by other scientists)</li></ol>\n<p>The iterative cycle inherent in this step-by-step method goes from point 3 to 6 back to 3 again.\n</p><p>While this schema outlines a typical hypothesis/testing method,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> it should also be noted that a number of philosophers, historians, and sociologists of science, including <a href="/wiki/Paul_Feyerabend" title="Paul Feyerabend">Paul Feyerabend</a>, claim that such descriptions of scientific method have little relation to the ways that science is actually practiced.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Characterizations">Characterizations</span></h3>\n<p>The scientific method depends upon increasingly sophisticated characterizations of the subjects of investigation. (The <i>subjects</i> can also be called <a href="/wiki/Category:Lists_of_unsolved_problems" title="Category:Lists of unsolved problems"><i>unsolved problems</i></a> or the <i>unknowns</i>.) For example, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> conjectured, correctly, that <a href="/wiki/St._Elmo%27s_fire" title="St. Elmo\'s fire">St. Elmo\'s fire</a> was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical" title="Electrical">electrical</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>, but it has taken a long series of experiments and theoretical changes to establish this. While seeking the pertinent properties of the subjects, careful thought may also <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence">entail</a> some definitions and observations; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observations" title="Observations">observations</a> often demand careful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Measurements" title="Measurements">measurements</a> and/or counting.\n</p><p>The systematic, careful collection of measurements or counts of relevant quantities is often the critical difference between <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudo-sciences</a>, such as alchemy, and science, such as chemistry or biology. Scientific measurements are usually tabulated, graphed, or mapped, and statistical manipulations, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correlation" title="Correlation">correlation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regression_analysis" title="Regression analysis">regression</a>, performed on them. The measurements might be made in a controlled setting, such as a laboratory, or made on more or less inaccessible or unmanipulatable objects such as stars or human populations. The measurements often require specialized <a href="/wiki/Scientific_instrument" title="Scientific instrument">scientific instruments</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectroscopes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Voltmeter" title="Voltmeter">voltmeters</a>, and the progress of a scientific field is usually intimately tied to their invention and improvement.\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I am not accustomed to saying anything with certainty after only one or two observations.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a>, (1546)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uncertainty">Uncertainty</span></h4>\n<p>Measurements in scientific work are also usually accompanied by estimates of their <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty" title="Uncertainty">uncertainty</a>. The uncertainty is often estimated by making repeated measurements of the desired quantity. Uncertainties may also be calculated by consideration of the uncertainties of the individual underlying quantities used. Counts of things, such as the number of people in a nation at a particular time, may also have an uncertainty due to data collection limitations. Or counts may represent a sample of desired quantities, with an uncertainty that depends upon the sampling method used and the number of samples taken.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h4>\n<p>Measurements demand the use of <i><a href="/wiki/Operational_definition" title="Operational definition">operational definitions</a></i> of relevant quantities. That is, a scientific quantity is described or defined by how it is measured, as opposed to some more vague, inexact or "idealized" definition. For example, <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a>, measured in amperes, may be operationally defined in terms of the mass of silver deposited in a certain time on an electrode in an electrochemical device that is described in some detail. The operational definition of a thing often relies on comparisons with standards: the operational definition of "mass" ultimately relies on the use of an artifact, such as a particular kilogram of platinum-iridium kept in a laboratory in France.\n</p><p>The scientific definition of a term sometimes differs substantially from its <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural language</a> usage. For example, <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weight" title="Weight">weight</a> overlap in meaning in common discourse, but have distinct meanings in <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a>. Scientific quantities are often characterized by their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement" title="Units of measurement">units of measure</a> which can later be described in terms of conventional physical units when communicating the work.\n</p><p>New theories are sometimes developed after realizing certain terms have not previously been sufficiently clearly defined. For example, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>\'s first paper on <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">relativity</a> begins by defining <a href="/wiki/Relativity_of_simultaneity" title="Relativity of simultaneity">simultaneity</a> and the means for determining <a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">length</a>. These ideas were skipped over by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> with, "I do not define <a href="/wiki/Time_in_physics#Galileo:_the_flow_of_time" title="Time in physics">time</a>, space, place and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motion_(physics)" title="Motion (physics)">motion</a>, as being well known to all." Einstein\'s paper then demonstrates that they (viz., absolute time and length independent of motion) were approximations. <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> cautions us that when characterizing a subject, however, it can be premature to define something when it remains ill-understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In Crick\'s study of <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>, he actually found it easier to study <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Visual_system" title="Visual system">visual system</a>, rather than to study <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>, for example. His cautionary example was the gene; the gene was much more poorly understood before Watson and Crick\'s pioneering discovery of the structure of DNA; it would have been counterproductive to spend much time on the definition of the gene, before them.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA-characterizations">DNA-characterizations</span></h4>\n<span style="vertical-align:-120%"><div class="floatleft"><img alt="" data-file-height="26" data-file-width="25" decoding="async" height="26" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/DNA_icon_%2825x25%29.png" width="25"/></div></span><p> The <a href="/wiki/DNA#History_of_DNA_research" title="DNA">history</a> of the discovery of the structure of DNA is a classic example of <a href="#Elements_of_the_scientific_method">the elements of the scientific method</a>: in 1950 it was known that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_inheritance" title="Genetic inheritance">genetic inheritance</a> had a mathematical description, starting with the studies of <a href="/wiki/Gregor_Mendel" title="Gregor Mendel">Gregor Mendel</a>, and that DNA contained genetic information (Oswald Avery\'s <i>transforming principle</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harvnb|McCarty1985_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-harvnb|McCarty1985-41">[41]</a></sup> But the mechanism of storing genetic information (i.e., genes) in DNA was unclear. Researchers in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_Bragg" title="William Lawrence Bragg">Bragg\'s</a> laboratory at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">Cambridge University</a> made <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> pictures of various <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, starting with <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a> of <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a>, and proceeding to more complicated substances. Using clues painstakingly assembled over decades, beginning with its chemical composition, it was determined that it should be possible to characterize the physical structure of DNA, and the X-ray images would be the vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method#DNA-hypotheses" title="Scientific method">..<i>2. DNA-hypotheses</i></a>\n</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Another_example:_precession_of_Mercury">Another example: precession of Mercury</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Perihelio.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Perihelio.svg/220px-Perihelio.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Perihelio.svg/330px-Perihelio.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Perihelio.svg/440px-Perihelio.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Perihelio.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Apsidal_precession" title="Apsidal precession">Precession</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion_and_aphelion" title="Perihelion and aphelion">perihelion</a> (exaggerated)</div></div></div>\n<p>The characterization element can require extended and extensive study, even centuries. It took thousands of years of measurements, from the <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldean</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">European</a> astronomers, to fully record the motion of planet <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. Newton was able to include those measurements into consequences of his <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton\'s laws of motion">laws of motion</a>. But the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion_and_aphelion" title="Perihelion and aphelion">perihelion</a> of the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> exhibits a precession that cannot be fully explained by Newton\'s laws of motion (see diagram to the right), as Leverrier pointed out in 1859. The observed difference for Mercury\'s <a href="/wiki/Apsidal_precession" title="Apsidal precession">precession</a> between Newtonian theory and observation was one of the things that occurred to <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> as a possible early test of his theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">General relativity</a>. His relativistic calculations matched observation much more closely than did Newtonian theory. The difference is approximately 43 arc-seconds per century.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypothesis_development">Hypothesis development</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypothesis_formation" title="Hypothesis formation">Hypothesis formation</a></div>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> is a suggested explanation of a phenomenon, or alternately a reasoned proposal suggesting a possible correlation between or among a set of phenomena.\n</p><p>Normally hypotheses have the form of a <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">mathematical model</a>. Sometimes, but not always, they can also be formulated as <a href="/wiki/Existential_quantification" title="Existential quantification">existential statements</a>, stating that some particular instance of the phenomenon being studied has some characteristic and causal explanations, which have the general form of <a href="/wiki/Universal_quantification" title="Universal quantification">universal statements</a>, stating that every instance of the phenomenon has a particular characteristic.\n</p><p>Scientists are free to use whatever resources they have &#8211; their own creativity, ideas from other fields, <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian inference</a>, and so on &#8211; to imagine possible explanations for a phenomenon under study. Albert Einstein once observed that "there is no logical bridge between phenomena and their theoretical principles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sanders_Peirce" title="Charles Sanders Peirce">Charles Sanders Peirce</a>, borrowing a page from <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Prior_Analytics" title="Prior Analytics">Prior Analytics</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Inquiry#Abduction" title="Inquiry">2.25</a>) described the incipient stages of <a href="/wiki/Inquiry" title="Inquiry">inquiry</a>, instigated by the "irritation of doubt" to venture a plausible guess, as <i><a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">abductive reasoning</a></i>. The history of science is filled with stories of scientists claiming a "flash of inspiration", or a hunch, which then motivated them to look for evidence to support or refute their idea. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Polanyi" title="Michael Polanyi">Michael Polanyi</a> made such creativity the centerpiece of his discussion of methodology.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Glen_(geologist_and_historian)" title="William Glen (geologist and historian)">William Glen</a> observes that<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the success of a hypothesis, or its service to science, lies not simply in its perceived "truth", or power to displace, subsume or reduce a predecessor idea, but perhaps more in its ability to stimulate the research that will illuminate ... bald suppositions and areas of vagueness.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In general scientists tend to look for theories that are "<a href="/wiki/Elegance" title="Elegance">elegant</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Beauty" title="Beauty">beautiful</a>". In contrast to the usual English use of these terms, they here refer to a theory in accordance with the known facts, which is nevertheless relatively simple and easy to handle. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occam%27s_Razor" title="Occam\'s Razor">Occam\'s Razor</a> serves as a rule of thumb for choosing the most desirable amongst a group of equally explanatory hypotheses.\n</p><p>To minimize the <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_bias" title="Confirmation bias">confirmation bias</a> which results from entertaining a single hypothesis, <a href="/wiki/Strong_inference" title="Strong inference">strong inference</a> emphasizes the need for entertaining multiple alternative hypotheses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platt_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Platt-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA-hypotheses">DNA-hypotheses</span></h4>\n<span style="vertical-align:-120%"><div class="floatleft"><img alt="" data-file-height="26" data-file-width="25" decoding="async" height="26" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/DNA_icon_%2825x25%29.png" width="25"/></div></span> <p><a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a> proposed that DNA might be a <a href="/wiki/Triple_helix" title="Triple helix">triple helix</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> This hypothesis was also considered by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_D._Watson" title="James D. Watson">James D. Watson</a> but discarded. When Watson and Crick learned of Pauling\'s hypothesis, they understood from existing data that Pauling was wrong<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and that Pauling would soon admit his difficulties with that structure. So, the race was on to figure out the correct structure (except that Pauling did not realize at the time that he was in a race) <i><a href="#DNA-predictions">..3. DNA-predictions</a></i>\n</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions_from_the_hypothesis">Predictions from the hypothesis</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prediction_in_science" title="Prediction in science">Prediction in science</a></div>\n<p>Any useful hypothesis will enable <a href="/wiki/Prediction" title="Prediction">predictions</a>, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> including <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductive reasoning</a>. It might predict the outcome of an experiment in a laboratory setting or the observation of a phenomenon in nature. The prediction can also be statistical and deal only with probabilities.\n</p><p>It is essential that the outcome of testing such a prediction be currently unknown. Only in this case does a successful outcome increase the probability that the hypothesis is true. If the outcome is already known, it is called a consequence and should have already been considered while <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method#Hypothesis_development" title="Scientific method">formulating the hypothesis</a>.\n</p><p>If the predictions are not accessible by observation or experience, the hypothesis is not yet testable and so will remain to that extent unscientific in a strict sense. A new technology or theory might make the necessary experiments feasible. For example, while a hypothesis on the existence of other intelligent species may be convincing with scientifically based speculation, there is no known experiment that can test this hypothesis. Therefore, science itself can have little to say about the possibility. In future, a new technique may allow for an experimental test and the speculation would then become part of accepted science.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA-predictions">DNA-predictions</span></h4>\n<span style="vertical-align:-120%"><div class="floatleft"><img alt="" data-file-height="26" data-file-width="25" decoding="async" height="26" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/DNA_icon_%2825x25%29.png" width="25"/></div></span> <p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_D._Watson" title="James D. Watson">James D. Watson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a>, and others hypothesized that DNA had a helical structure. This implied that DNA\'s X-ray diffraction pattern would be \'x shaped\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crick_pp._137&#8211;138_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crick_pp._137&#8211;138-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> This prediction followed from the work of Cochran, Crick and Vand<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HelixTransform_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-HelixTransform-45">[45]</a></sup> (and independently by Stokes). The Cochran-Crick-Vand-Stokes theorem provided a mathematical explanation for the empirical observation that diffraction from helical structures produces x shaped patterns.\n</p><p>In their first paper, Watson and Crick also noted that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_helix" title="Double helix">double helix</a> structure they proposed provided a simple mechanism for <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>, writing, "It has not escaped our notice that the specific pairing we have postulated immediately suggests a possible copying mechanism for the genetic material".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="#DNA-experiments"><i> ..4. DNA-experiments</i></a>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Another_example:_general_relativity">Another example: general relativity</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gravitational_lens-full.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="702" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Gravitational_lens-full.jpg/200px-Gravitational_lens-full.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Gravitational_lens-full.jpg/300px-Gravitational_lens-full.jpg 1.5x,
