Al-Battani is known for his accurate observations at al-Raqqah in Syria, beginning in 877. His work includes measurement of the annual precession of the equinoxes.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcElheny2004">McElheny 2004</a> p. 53: The weekend (January 31 &#8211; February 1) after seeing photo 51, Watson informed Bragg of the X-ray diffraction image of DNA in B form. Bragg gave them permission to restart their research on DNA (that is, model building).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcElheny2004">McElheny 2004</a> p. 54: On Sunday February 8, 1953, Maurice Wilkes gave Watson and Crick permission to work on models, as Wilkes would not be building models until Franklin left DNA research.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcElheny2004">McElheny 2004</a> p. 56: <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Donohue" title="Jerry Donohue">Jerry Donohue</a>, on sabbatical from Pauling\'s lab and visiting Cambridge, advises Watson that textbook form of the base pairs was incorrect for DNA base pairs; rather, the keto form of the base pairs should be used instead. This form allowed the bases\' hydrogen bonds to pair \'unlike\' with \'unlike\', rather than to pair \'like\' with \'like\', as Watson was inclined to model, on the basis of the textbook statements. On February 27, 1953, Watson was convinced enough to make cardboard models of the nucleotides in their keto form.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">\n"Suddenly I became aware that an <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>-<a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a> pair held together by two <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> was identical in shape to a <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a>-<a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> pair held together by at least two hydrogen bonds. ..." &#8211; <a href="#CITEREFWatson1968">Watson 1968</a>, pp.&#160;194&#8211;97.\n<ul><li><a href="#CITEREFMcElheny2004">McElheny 2004</a> p. 57 Saturday, February 28, 1953, Watson tried \'like with like\' and admitted these base pairs didn\'t have hydrogen bonds that line up. But after trying \'unlike with unlike\', and getting <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Donohue" title="Jerry Donohue">Jerry Donohue</a>\'s approval, the base pairs turned out to be identical in shape (as Watson stated above in his 1968 <i>Double Helix</i> memoir quoted above). Watson now felt confident enough to inform Crick. (Of course, \'unlike with unlike\' increases the number of possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codon" title="Codon">codons</a>, if this scheme were a <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a>.)</li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">\nSee, e.g., <i>Physics Today</i>, <b>59</b>(1), p. 42. <a class="external text" href="http://ptonline.aip.org/journals/doc/PHTOAD-ft/vol_59/iss_1/42_1.shtml?bypassSSO=1" rel="nofollow">Richmann electrocuted in St. Petersburg (1753)</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">\n<a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Prior_Analytics" title="Prior Analytics">Prior Analytics</a>", <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hugh_Tredennick&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hugh Tredennick (page does not exist)">Hugh Tredennick</a> (trans.), pp. 181&#8211;531 in <i>Aristotle, Volume&#160;1</i>, <a href="/wiki/Loeb_Classical_Library" title="Loeb Classical Library">Loeb Classical Library</a>, William Heinemann, London, 1938.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Doubt-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Doubt_92-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"What one does not in the least doubt one should not pretend to doubt; but a man should train himself to doubt," said Peirce in a brief intellectual autobiography; see Ketner, Kenneth Laine (2009) "Charles Sanders Peirce: Interdisciplinary Scientist" in <i>The Logic of Interdisciplinarity</i>). Peirce held that actual, genuine doubt originates externally, usually in surprise, but also that it is to be sought and cultivated, "provided only that it be the weighty and noble metal itself, and no counterfeit nor paper substitute"; in "Issues of Pragmaticism", <i>The Monist</i>, v. XV, n. 4, pp. 481&#8211;99, see <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/monistquart15hegeuoft#page/484/mode/1up" rel="nofollow">p. 484</a>, and <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/monistquart15hegeuoft#page/491/mode/1up" rel="nofollow">p. 491</a>. (Reprinted in <i>Collected Papers</i> v. 5, paragraphs 438&#8211;63, see 443 and 451).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-93">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">But see <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method_and_religion" title="Scientific method and religion">Scientific method and religion</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Peirce (1898), "Philosophy and the Conduct of Life", Lecture 1 of the Cambridge (MA) Conferences Lectures, published in <i>Collected Papers</i> v. 1, paragraphs 616&#8211;48 in part and in <i>Reasoning and the Logic of Things</i>, Ketner (ed., intro.) and Putnam (intro., comm.), pp. 105&#8211;22, reprinted in <i>Essential Peirce</i> v. 2, pp. 27&#8211;41.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-F.R.L.-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-F.R.L._95-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">" ... in order to learn, one must desire to learn ..." &#8211; Peirce (1899), "F.R.L." Consequently, the art of discovery is purely a question of economics. The economics of research is, so far as logic is concerned, the leading doctrine with reference to the art of discovery. Consequently, the conduct of abduction, which is chiefly a question of heuretic and is the first question of heuretic, is to be governed by economical considerations.\n</p></blockquote></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-LogAbduc-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-LogAbduc_102-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Peirce (1903), "Pragmatism &#8211; The Logic of Abduction", <i>Collected Papers</i> v. 5, paragraphs 195&#8211;205, especially 196. <a class="external text" href="http://www.textlog.de/7663.html" rel="nofollow">Eprint</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-20Qs-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20Qs_103-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Peirce, "On the Logic of Drawing Ancient History from Documents", <i>Essential Peirce</i> v. 2, see pp. 107&#8211;09.
